Interpretation: We have a sequence of positive integers, each term (a_n) has at least three proper divisors. Then a_{n+1} = sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. We need to determine all possible values of a1 such that the sequence is infinite (i.e., the definition holds for all n ≥ 1; but the sequence is defined recursively, and we need that for each n, a_n has at least three proper divisors, so that a_{n+1} is defined as described). For N = 6k, the divisors >1 include 2,3,4 (if 4|N),5 (if 5|N),6 (always, because 6 divides N),7 (if 7|N), etc. The ordering among them depends on the size of primes and exponents. So d4 is the third smallest divisor >1. Since 2 and 3 are always divisors, the third smallest will be the minimum of the set {4,5,6,7,...} that actually divide N. - N divisible by 4: d4 = 4.
- N not divisible by 4, but divisible by 5: d4 = 5.
- N not divisible by 4 and not divisible by 5: d4 = 6.

But what about the case where N is divisible by 4 and also by 5? That's covered by the first case, d4 = 4. So it's consistent.

Thus, f(N) = 5k + (6k)/d4, where d4 is as above.

Now, (6k)/d4 is integer because d4 divides N = 6k. So f(N) = 5k + (6k)/d4.

Let's try to compute f(N) for N multiples of 6 and see a pattern: f(N) = 5k + (6k)/d4. If N is divisible by 4, then d4=4, so f = 5k + (6k)/4 = 5k + (3k)/2 = (10k + 3k)/2 = 13k/2. If N is not divisible by 4 or 5, then d4=6, so f = 5k + (6k)/6 = 5k + k = 6k = N. So f(N)=N, which is multiple of 6. So fixed points.

Then the sequence is strictly decreasing. So it cannot be infinite, because strictly decreasing positive integers must eventually reach 1, and 1 has 0 proper divisors, so the sequence would stop (a_n would be undefined for some n). Strictly decreasing positive integers cannot be infinite because they would have to go to 0 or negative, but they are positive, so they can only have finitely many terms before reaching 1.    Actually we can prove a stronger inequality: For any N > 1 with at least 3 proper divisors, let p be smallest prime divisor. Then g(N) = N/p + N/q + N/r. It might be easier to bound using the fact that p,q,r are at least 2,3,4 (but not always). But we can do casework.

   So Lemma 1 holds.

   Additionally, we need to prove that if N is not a multiple of 6, then g(N) is not a multiple of 6. That's essentially a corollary of the above with additional parity/mod3 arguments. Let's prove:

   Now, the induction: Suppose a1 is not a multiple of 6. Then a2 = g(a1) is not a multiple of 6. Also, a2 < a1.    Now, we also need to prove that if N is not a multiple of 6, then g(N) is not a multiple of 6. That is: either g(N) is odd (if N odd) or g(N) is not divisible by 3 (if N even not multiple of 3).    So indeed, for even N not multiple of 3, g(N) is not a multiple of 3. Therefore, g(N) is not a multiple of 6.

   Thus, Lemma 2 is proved.

   Now, we can proceed.

   The first part: a1 must be a multiple of 6.

   So we need to analyze which multiples of 6 lead to infinite sequence.

   Now, for the sequence to be infinite, we need all a_n to have at least 3 proper divisors. Since a1 is multiple of 6, it's fine. The recursion is a_{n+1} = g(a_n). We need to examine the orbit.

   If a1 is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5, then a2 = a1 (constant), so all terms are multiples of 6, and they have at least 3 proper divisors. So infinite.

   If a1 is a multiple of 6 and divisible by 4, then a2 = 13*(a1/12). Since a2 is a multiple of 6, we need to examine whether a2 is in the "good" set. If a2 is not good, then the sequence might die later. But we are looking for a1 such that the entire sequence exists. So we need to determine conditions on a1 (multiple of 6) that guarantee that all subsequent terms are multiples of 6 and have at least 3 proper divisors. That seems to be: a1 must be a multiple of 6, and either (i) a1 is not divisible by 4 or 5 (fixed point), or (ii) a1 is a multiple of 72 and a1/72 is odd and not divisible by 5. Let's read the problem statement again carefully: "The infinite sequence a_1, a_2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. For each n ≥ 1, the integer a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. Determine all possible values of a_1." It doesn't explicitly say that the sequence is defined for all n (i.e., that we can always compute a_{n+1} from a_n). The phrase "The infinite sequence ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors." That is a condition on the sequence. Then "For each n ≥ 1, the integer a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n." So we are to find all a_1 for which there exists an infinite sequence (a_n) with that property.    - For n ≥ 1, a_n has at least 3 proper divisors. Then define b_n = sum of three largest proper divisors of a_n. Then a_{n+1} = b_n.
   - Need to find all a_1 such that the infinite sequence exists.

   Proof approach:

   1. Prove that if a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence cannot be infinite. (We'll show that a_2 < a_1 and a_2 is not a multiple of 6, then by induction all a_n are not multiples of 6 and strictly decreasing → eventually a_n = 1 or a prime, contradiction. Also need to prove that if N is not a multiple of 6, then g(N) is not a multiple of 6. That can be derived from the decrease and the fact that if g(N) were a multiple of 6, then we could get a contradiction? Actually easier: Show that if N is not a multiple of 6, then either N is odd (then g(N) is odd, so not multiple of 6) or N is even but not divisible by 3 (then we prove that g(N) is not divisible by 3). That's enough to conclude that g(N) is not a multiple of 6. But we also need to prove that if a1 is not a multiple of 6, then all subsequent terms are not multiples of 6. That follows from the fact that if a term is not a multiple of 6, then its image is not a multiple of 6. So we need to prove that property: If N is not a multiple of 6, then g(N) is not a multiple of 6. And also that g(N) < N (for strict decrease). But we also need to prove that if a1 is not a multiple of 6, then a2 is defined (since a1 has at least 3 proper divisors, given). Then a2 < a1. Also, a2 is not a multiple of 6. Then by induction, all a_n are not multiples of 6 and strictly decreasing. So after finitely many steps, we will hit a number with < 3 proper divisors. That's a contradiction with the existence of an infinite sequence (since the sequence would stop). Hence, a1 must be a multiple of 6.

   So step 1: prove Lemma 1 (for N with at least 3 proper divisors): 
       - If N is odd, then g(N) is odd.
       - If N is even and not a multiple of 3, then g(N) is not a multiple of 3.
       - Additionally, in both cases g(N) < N. (We'll prove this via inequality.)

   Actually we can prove a stronger inequality: For any N > 1 with at least 3 proper divisors, let p be smallest prime divisor. Then g(N) = N/p + N/q + N/r. It might be easier to bound using the fact that p,q,r are at least 2,3,4 (but not always). But we can do casework.

   Let's prove:

That's ≤ N/2 + N/5 + N/(2*5) = 0.5N + 0.2N + 0.1N = 0.8N. So < N. So okay.

        * If e ≥ 2, then N is divisible by 4. Then d2 = 2, d3 = 4. Then d4 is the next smallest divisor > 4. We need to bound g(N) < N. Consider the possibilities:

            - If e ≥ 3, then 8 is a divisor, so d4 = 8. Then g(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/8 = 7N/8 < N.

   So Lemma 1 holds.

   Additionally, we need to prove that if N is not a multiple of 6, then g(N) is not a multiple of 6. That's essentially a corollary of the above with additional parity/mod3 arguments. Let's prove:

   So Lemma 2: If N is not a multiple of 6, then g(N) is not a multiple of 6.

   Now, the induction: Suppose a1 is not a multiple of 6. Then a2 = g(a1) is not a multiple of 6. Also, a2 < a1.    Now, we also need to prove that if N is not a multiple of 6, then g(N) is not a multiple of 6. That is: either g(N) is odd (if N odd) or g(N) is not divisible by 3 (if N even not multiple of 3).    So indeed, for even N not multiple of 3, g(N) is not a multiple of 3. Therefore, g(N) is not a multiple of 6.

   Thus, Lemma 2 is proved.

   Now, we can proceed.

   The first part: a1 must be a multiple of 6.

## Solution
We need to determine all possible values of the first term \(a_1\) of an infinite sequence of positive integers where each term has at least three proper divisors and for \(n\ge1\) the next term is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of the previous term.

Let \(S(N)\) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a positive integer \(N\).  The sequence is defined by \(a_{n+1}=S(a_n)\) for \(n\ge1\), and we are given that each \(a_n\) has at least three proper divisors.

---

### 1.  Expressions for \(S(N)\)

For any \(N>1\) with at least three proper divisors we list its positive divisors in increasing order:

\[
1=d_1<d_2<\dots<d_k=N .
\]

Because there are at least four divisors (the three proper divisors and \(N\) itself), \(d_2,d_3,d_4\) are well defined.  The mapping \(d\mapsto N/d\) is an involution on the set of divisors.  The three largest proper divisors are precisely \(N/d_2,\ N/d_3,\ N/d_4\) (see the proof below).

**Proof.**  
Let \(x\) be a proper divisor of \(N\).  Write \(x=N/d\) with a divisor \(d>1\).  The ordering \(d_2\le d_3\le d_4\) implies \(N/d_2\ge N/d_3\ge N/d_4\).  If a proper divisor \(y\) were larger than \(N/d_4\), then \(y=N/d\) for some divisor \(d\) with \(d<d_4\); but the only divisors \(<d_4\) are \(d_2\) and \(d_3\) (and \(1\), which is not allowed because \(d>1\)).  Hence \(d\in\{d_2,d_3\}\) and \(y\) is one of \(N/d_2,\ N/d_3\).  Consequently \(N/d_4\) is the third largest proper divisor.  So the three largest are \(N/d_2,\ N/d_3,\ N/d_4\).

Thus

\[
S(N)=\frac{N}{d_2}+\frac{N}{d_3}+\frac{N}{d_4}.
\]

---

### 2.  Necessary condition: \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\)

**Lemma 1.**  If \(N\) is **not** a multiple of \(6\) and has at least three proper divisors, then \(S(N)<N\) and \(S(N)\) is **not** a multiple of \(6\).

*Proof.*  We distinguish two cases.

*Case 1 – \(N\) is odd.*  Then all divisors are odd; therefore each of the three terms \(N/d_2,N/d_3,N/d_4\) is odd and their sum is odd, so \(S(N)\) is odd.  In particular \(S(N)\) is not a multiple of \(2\), hence not a multiple of \(6\).  
Also, because \(N\) is odd, its smallest prime divisor \(p\) is at least \(3\).  If \(3\mid N\) then \(p=3\) and the next divisors are \(\ge5\); if \(3\nmid N\) then \(p\ge5\).  In either subcase we have

\[
\frac{N}{d_2}+\frac{N}{d_3}+\frac{N}{d_4}\le\frac{N}{3}+\frac{N}{5}+\frac{N}{5}=\frac{11}{15}N<N .
\]

If \(3\nmid N\) then \(p\ge5\) and the bound is \(\frac{3}{5}N<N\).  Hence \(S(N)<N\).

*Case 2 – \(N\) is even but not a multiple of \(3\).*  Write \(N=2^e\cdot m\) with \(m\) odd and \(\gcd(2,m)=1\).  Since \(3\nmid N\), we have \(\gcd(3,N)=1\).  The three smallest divisors \(>1\) are:

- If \(e=1\) then \(d_2=2\) and the next divisor is the smallest odd prime divisor of \(N\) (call it \(p\)); because \(3\nmid N\) we have \(p\ge5\).  Then \(d_3=p\) and \(d_4=2p\).  Consequently

\[
S(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{p}+\frac{N}{2p}=m+\frac{2m}{p}+\frac{m}{p}=m+\frac{3m}{p}.
\]

Since \(p\ge5\) we have \(S(N)\le m+\frac{3m}{5}=\frac{8}{5}m\).  Also \(N=2m\); thus \(S(N)<N\) because \(m+\frac{3m}{p}<2m\) (the inequality is strict because \(p\ge5\) forces \(\frac{3m}{p}\le\frac{3m}{5}\) and if equality held we would need \(p=5\) and then \(S(N)=m+\frac{3m}{5}= \frac{8}{5}m<2m\)).  

If \(e\ge2\) then \(4\mid N\) and \(d_2=2,\ d_3=3\) (because \(3\nmid N\) is not guaranteed? Actually \(N\) is even and not a multiple of \(3\), but we need to know whether \(3\) divides \(N\). In this case \(N\) is even but not a multiple of \(3\), so \(3\) does not divide \(N\).  However we cannot claim that \(d_3=3\) because \(3\) may not be a divisor.  Let's carefully treat the subcase \(e\ge2\) with the given that \(N\) is not a multiple of \(3\).  

We need to compute the three smallest divisors \(>1\) when \(N\) is even and not divisible by \(3\).  The smallest divisor is \(2\).  The next divisor could be \(4\) if \(N\) is divisible by \(4\), otherwise it could be an odd prime \(p\ge5\).  So we have to split:

- If \(N\) is divisible by \(4\) (i.e. \(e\ge2\)), then \(d_2=2\), \(d_3=4\).  The next divisor \(d_4\) is the smallest divisor \(>4\) that divides \(N\).  It could be \(8\) (if \(e\ge3\)) or some odd prime \(q\ge5\) (if \(N\) is divisible by such prime).  Then

\[
S(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{4}+\frac{N}{d_4}= \frac{3N}{4}+\frac{N}{d_4}.
\]

Since \(d_4\ge5\) and \(N\) is not a multiple of \(3\), we have \(\frac{N}{d_4}\) not divisible by \(3\).  Moreover \(\frac{3N}{4}\) is an integer and is divisible by \(3\) because \(N/4\) is integer.  Therefore

\[
S(N)\equiv \frac{N}{d_4}\pmod{3},
\]

and \(\frac{N}{d_4}\) is not a multiple of \(3\); hence \(S(N)\) is not a multiple of \(3\).  Also \(S(N)\) is even (the sum of an even number and an integer).  So \(S(N)\) is not a multiple of \(6\) (because it is not a multiple of \(3\)).

- If \(N\) is not divisible by \(4\) (i.e. \(e=1\)), then as already considered \(S(N)=m+\frac{3m}{p}\) with \(p\ge5\).  Since \(m\) is not divisible by \(3\) (otherwise \(N\) would be a multiple of \(3\)), we have \(S(N)\equiv m\pmod{3}\) and \(m\) is not a multiple of \(3\).  Hence \(S(N)\) is not a multiple of \(3\).  So again \(S(N)\) is not a multiple of \(6\).

Thus in every subcase \(S(N)\) is not a multiple of \(6\) and \(S(N)<N\).

∎

Now suppose \(a_1\) is **not** a multiple of \(6\).  Then by Lemma 1, \(a_2=S(a_1)<a_1\) and \(a_2\) is not a multiple of \(6\).  Because the sequence is assumed to be infinite, each term has at least three proper divisors.  Hence \(a_2\) has at least three proper divisors and is not a multiple of \(6\); therefore Lemma 1 applies to \(a_2\) as well, giving \(a_3<S(a_2)<a_2\) and \(a_3\) not a multiple of \(6\).  Continuing we obtain an infinite strictly decreasing sequence of positive integers, which is impossible.  Consequently such an \(a_1\) cannot be the first term of an infinite sequence.  Thus

\[
\boxed{a_1\text{ must be a multiple of }6}.
\]

---

### 3.  Behaviour of \(S(N)\) for multiples of \(6\)

Let \(N\) be a multiple of \(6\).  Write its prime factorisation as

\[
N=2^a\cdot3^b\cdot m,\qquad \gcd(m,6)=1,\; a\ge1,\; b\ge1 .
\]

We determine the three smallest divisors \(>1\).

*If \(a\ge2\)* then \(4\mid N\) and the divisors are

\[
2,\;3,\;4.
\]

Thus \(d_2=2,\ d_3=3,\ d_4=4\) and

\[
S(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{3}+\frac{N}{4}= \frac{13N}{12}.
\]

*If \(a=1\)* then \(N=2\cdot3^b\cdot m\).  The divisors \(>1\) are \(2,3,\dots\).  The next divisor after \(3\) is the smallest integer \(>3\) that divides \(N\).  Because \(6=2\cdot3\) divides \(N\), we have \(6\) as a candidate.  If \(5\mid N\) then \(5\) is also a divisor and is smaller than \(6\); otherwise \(6\) is the smallest.  Therefore

\[
\begin{cases}
d_4=5 &\text{if }5\mid N,\\[2mm]
d_4=6 &\text{if }5\nmid N.
\end{cases}
\]

Hence

\[
S(N)=\begin{cases}
\displaystyle\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{3}+\frac{N}{5}= \frac{31N}{30}, &\text{if }5\mid N,\\[4mm]
\displaystyle\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{3}+\frac{N}{6}= N, &\text{if }5\nmid N.
\end{cases}
\]

(When \(5\mid N\) we have \(N\) divisible by \(30\); then \(N/30\) is an integer, so the formula is valid.)

---

### 4.  Determining all possible \(a_1\) among multiples of \(6\)

We assume \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\).  The sequence will be infinite only if every term has at least three proper divisors, which is automatically true for multiples of \(6\) (they are \(>2\) and have the proper divisors \(1,2,3\) at least).  The crucial point is that if any term becomes a number that is **not** a multiple of \(6\), then by Lemma 1 the subsequent terms would be strictly decreasing and the sequence could not be infinite.  Hence for the sequence to be infinite we must have

\[
a_n\text{ is a multiple of }6\quad\text{for all }n.
\]

Equivalently, all iterates must stay in the set of multiples of \(6\).  We now examine the orbits of the function \(S\) on multiples of \(6\).

---

#### 4.1  Numbers that are **not** divisible by \(4\) and **not** divisible by \(5\)

If \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\) and is **not** divisible by \(4\) and **not** divisible by \(5\), then by the formula for \(a=1\) and \(5\nmid N\) we have \(S(a_1)=a_1\).  Thus the sequence is constant, all terms are equal to \(a_1\), and each term has at least three proper divisors.  Hence such \(a_1\) are admissible.

---

#### 4.2  Numbers that are divisible by \(4\)

Suppose \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\) and divisible by \(4\).  Then \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(12\).  From the formula for \(a\ge2\) we obtain \(S(a_1)=\dfrac{13a_1}{12}\).  For the sequence to be infinite we need that \(a_2\) is again a multiple of \(6\).  Hence \(\dfrac{13a_1}{12}\) must be a multiple of \(6\).  Because \(13\) is coprime to \(6\), this is equivalent to

\[
\frac{a_1}{12}\text{ is a multiple of }6,
\]

i.e. \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(72\).  Therefore any admissible \(a_1\) that is divisible by \(4\) must be a multiple of \(72\).  Write

\[
a_1=72s,\qquad s\in\mathbb{N}.
\]

Then

\[
a_2=S(72s)=\frac{13\cdot72s}{12}=78s.
\]

Now \(a_2\) is a multiple of \(6\).  We need to examine whether the orbit can continue indefinitely.  The only way the sequence could become finite is if at some step we obtain a term that is not a multiple of \(6\).  Since we already know that if a term is not a multiple of \(6\) the sequence dies (Lemma 1), we must ensure that all terms stay multiples of \(6\).  This will be the case iff we never encounter a term that is a multiple of \(5\) (because if a term is a multiple of \(5\) and also a multiple of \(6\), the next term is \(31N/30\), which is not a multiple of \(6\)).  Consequently every term must be **not** divisible by \(5\).

Thus the orbit from \(a_1=72s\) is infinite iff:

- \(a_2=78s\) is a multiple of \(6\) (always) and **not** divisible by \(5\);
- moreover, when we compute \(a_3=S(a_2)\) we must again obtain a multiple of \(6\) that is not divisible by \(5\); the recursion continues.

Now we analyse the possibilities for \(s\).

---

#### 4.2.1  The parameter \(s\) is **not** a multiple of \(5\)

If \(5\nmid s\), then \(a_2=78s\) is not divisible by \(5\) (since \(78\) is not divisible by \(5\)).  We also need that \(a_2\) is not divisible by \(4\)?  Actually if \(a_2\) is divisible by \(4\) then the next term \(a_3\) will be \(13a_2/12\), which may or may not be a multiple of \(6\).  But we also have the condition that we must avoid multiples of \(5\) in the orbit.  Since \(a_2\) is not a multiple of \(5\), the only danger is that later terms become multiples of \(5\).  Let's compute \(a_3=S(78s)\).

Now \(78s\) is a multiple of \(6\).  To apply the formulas we need to know whether \(78s\) is divisible by \(4\) or not.  Write \(78s=2\cdot3\cdot13\cdot s\).  The exponent of \(2\) in \(78s\) is \(1\) plus the exponent of \(2\) in \(s\).  Hence:

- If \(s\) is **odd**, then \(78s\) has exactly one factor \(2\) → not divisible by \(4\).
- If \(s\) is **even**, then \(78s\) is divisible by \(4\).

Therefore we have two subcases.

**Subcase 4.2.1.a  – \(s\) is odd and \(5\nmid s\).**  
Then \(78s\) is not divisible by \(4\) and not divisible by \(5\).  According to the formula for \(a=1\) and \(5\nmid N\) (since \(5\nmid s\) and \(5\nmid78\)), we have \(S(78s)=78s\).  Hence \(a_3=a_2\) and the sequence becomes constant from that point onward.  Since all terms are multiples of \(6\) and not multiples of \(5\), the orbit is infinite.  So any such \(s\) yields an admissible \(a_1=72s\).

**Subcase 4.2.1.b  – \(s\) is even and \(5\nmid s\).**  
Then \(78s\) is divisible by \(4\).  Write \(78s\) as a multiple of \(12\) (it is).  Then \(S(78s)=\dfrac{13\cdot78s}{12}= \dfrac{13\cdot13}{2}s = \dfrac{169}{2}s\).  Because \(s\) is even, this is an integer.  Now we need to check whether \(a_3\) is a multiple of \(6\) and not a multiple of \(5\).  Compute modulo \(6\):

\[
\frac{169}{2}s \equiv \frac{169}{2}s \pmod{6}.
\]

Since \(169\equiv1\pmod{12}\), we have \(\frac{169}{2}s = \frac{169s}{2}\) and \(169s\equiv s\pmod{12}\) (because \(169\equiv1\)).  For \(\frac{169s}{2}\) to be an integer, \(s\) must be even; that's satisfied.  The condition that \(a_3\) is a multiple of \(6\) is equivalent to

\[
\frac{169s}{2}\equiv0\pmod{6}\;\Longleftrightarrow\; 169s\equiv0\pmod{12}\;\Longleftrightarrow\; s\equiv0\pmod{12}
\]

because \(169\equiv1\pmod{12}\).  Hence if \(s\) is a multiple of \(12\), then \(a_3\) is a multiple of \(6\); otherwise \(a_3\) is not a multiple of \(6\) and the sequence dies (since then \(a_3\) would not be a multiple of \(6\) and by Lemma 1 the orbit would become decreasing and finite).  But we also need that \(a_3\) is **not** a multiple of \(5\).  That depends on \(s\): if \(s\) is a multiple of \(5\) then \(a_3\) is a multiple of \(5\) (since \(169\) is not divisible by \(5\)), but we are in the case \(5\nmid s\), so \(a_3\) is not a multiple of \(5\).  Thus the only dangerous situation is when \(s\) is even but not a multiple of \(12\); then \(a_3\) is not a multiple of \(6\) and the sequence terminates.

Now suppose \(s\) is a multiple of \(12\) (i.e. \(s=12t\)).  Then \(a_3\) is a multiple of \(6\).  Moreover \(a_3\) is not a multiple of \(5\) because \(s\) is not a multiple of \(5\).  Now we need to examine whether the orbit can continue beyond \(a_3\).  Compute \(a_3\) explicitly: \(a_3 = \dfrac{169}{2}s = \dfrac{169}{2}\cdot12t = 169\cdot6t = 1014t\).  So \(a_3=1014t\).  Note that \(1014=2\cdot3\cdot13^2\).  Thus \(a_3\) is a multiple of \(6\).  Also, \(a_3\) is not divisible by \(5\) (since \(t\) not multiple of \(5\) if we want to avoid that).  Now we need to see whether \(a_3\) is divisible by \(4\) or not.  Write \(1014t = 2\cdot3\cdot13^2\cdot t\).  The exponent of \(2\) in \(a_3\) is \(1\) plus the exponent of \(2\) in \(t\).  So:

- If \(t\) is **odd**, then \(a_3\) is not divisible by \(4\) → \(a_3\) is a fixed point (since also \(5\nmid a_3\) if \(t\) not multiple of \(5\)).  Then the sequence becomes constant.
- If \(t\) is **even**, then \(a_3\) is divisible by \(4\).  Then we need to check whether it is a multiple of \(72\) to continue.  This leads to a recursion that reduces the parameter in some way.  But we can also notice that the overall transformation on \(s\) (starting from \(a_1=72s\)) is akin to a Euclidean algorithm; the orbit will eventually either reach a fixed point or encounter a multiple of \(5\) causing death.  We need a full description.

The example \(s=12\) (which is a multiple of \(12\) and odd? Actually \(12\) is even, but it's a multiple of \(12\) and also even.  For \(s=12\), \(t=1\) (odd), so \(a_3=1014\), which is a fixed point.  That's okay.  For \(s=24\) (multiple of \(12\), even), \(t=2\) (even).  Then \(a_3=2028\), which is divisible by \(4\).  Then we compute \(a_4 = S(2028) = \dfrac{13\cdot2028}{12}=13\cdot169 = 2197\).  \(2197\) is not a multiple of \(6\) (it is \(13^3\)), so the sequence dies.  So \(s=24\) fails.

Thus the condition for admissibility is not simply that \(s\) is not a multiple of \(5\) and is odd.  We need to determine all \(s\) for which the orbit never leaves multiples of \(6\).  This seems like we need to solve the recurrence.

---

#### 4.2.2  The parameter \(s\) is a multiple of \(5\)

If \(5\mid s\) then \(a_2=78s\) is divisible by \(5\).  Since also it is a multiple of \(6\), the next term \(a_3\) will be \(\
